 HDFriday's Top Stories in the United States and Mexico      WC817 words   PD21 January 2008   ET07:35   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.

     * Next Wk/US:  The week ahead is shortened by the M.L. King, Jr. 
Birthday holiday Monday, probably quite welcome considering the dearth 
of economic data on the calendar and absence of Fed officials from the 
public space during the press blackout period prior to the Jan. 29-30 
FOMC meeting. 
-- The only data of note will be the NAR's December report on existing 
home sales Thursday. Despite a slight uptick in the November numbers, 
the outlook for the housing market remains grim with an overabundance of 
homes on the market and an underabundance of credit with which to 
purchase them. 
-- The Richmond Fed surveys of manufacturing and services for January 
will be on Tuesday.  The weekly measures of retail activity will be
shifted to Wednesday due to the holiday. 
-- Weekly initial jobless claims will offer a revision to the survey 
comparison week. Data for early January can be erratic, so this may not 
be a good comparison of the fundamentals. 
-- The  Bank of Canada is widely expected to cut the overnight rate by 
25 basis points to 4.00% on Tuesday. The Bank of Japan governors meet on 
Jan. 21-22, and no change is expected in rates. The Reserve Bank of New 
Zealand meets on Jan. 24 and no change is anticipated. 

     * Treasuriess gained in short session Friday: On a 3:00 p.m. Thu to 
1:00 pm ET Fri basis close, the cash 2-year note yield declined 8.7 bps 
to 2.342%. The 5-year yield fell 4.8 bps to 2.85%. The 10-year yield 
rose 1.0 bps to 3.649%, and the 30-year yield rose 4.6  bps to  to 
4.299%. The 2-year/5-year curve steepened 3.9 bps to +50.8 bps, the 
2-year/10-year also steepened 9.7 bps to +130.7 0 bps, and the 
2-year/30-year steepened 13.3 bps to +195.7 bps. 

     * The dollar was trading with a mixed tone as the early close of 
the abbreviated U.S. trading session approached, holding slight gains 
vs. the euro but suffering modest losses vs. the yen. Euro-dollar was 
trading at $1.4628 ahead of 1 p.m. EST Friday, not much changed from its 
$1.4645 starting level of the session and about the middle of a 
$1.4603/1.4696 U.S. session range. Dollar-yen meantime was trading at 
Y106.95, below its Y107.46 starting level of the session at the lower 
end of its Y106.80/107.50 range seen in U.S. hours. 

     * The stars in Washington appear to be lining up for a quick 
agreement between President Bush and congressional leaders on an 
economic stimulus package of about $145 billion. Bush's comments Friday 
on the outlines of an economic stimulus package culminated a busy week 
in which leaders from both parties set aside personal mistrust and 
philosophical differences in an attempt to reach an agreement on a 
stimulus plan to shore up the weakening U.S. economy. Bush said a 
package of about 1% of gross domestic product--about $145 billion--would 
be adequate to give a "shot in the arm to keep a fundamentally sound 
economy healthy." 

     * The U.S. economy has gotten worse and may slump further, which 
could require the Federal Reserve to cut interest rates again, Richmond 
Federal Reserve Bank President Jeffrey Lacker said Friday. The Fed has 
already lowered the federal funds rate considerably in response to the 
economic slowdown. But more weakness could prompt further action, said 
Lacker, an alternate Federal Open Market Committee voter for 2008. -- 
Lacker Friday said that he's now more willing to look at further 
interest rate cuts because of new, weak economic data. 

     * Top U.S. bank economists expect the Federal Reserve to cut short 
term interest rates by 50 basis points at the upcoming FOMC meeting, and 
by another 25 basis points in both March and April. They also projected 
Friday that the growth of output and employment will be well below trend 
this year, but there will be a noticeable pickup in the second half, the 
Economic Advisory Council Committee of the American Bankers Association 
said Friday. 

     * Touted as an integral measure to prop up the unsettled housing 
market, the Treasury Department's "Hope Now" program has not fully 
caught the attention of those it aims to help, troubled homeowners 
facing foreclosure, yet there is potential for progress in the weeks 
ahead, an alliance report Friday showed.  

     * The Bank of Mexico kept monetary policy on hold as expected 
Friday, leaving the key overnight rate at 7.50%, but expressed 
continuing concerns about rising food prices and the impact of the U.S. 
slowdown on the Mexican economy.

 HDFed Cuts Rates Aggressively, Sets Stage for More Next Week      WC1,240 words   PD22 January 2008   ET16:16   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD

By Steven K. Beckner
               
     Market News International - In the wake of a global market collapse 
that portended further heavy losses of equity wealth in the United 
States, the Federal Reserve chose to act immediately and aggressively 
before U.S. markets opened Tuesday morning to protect the economy, 
rather than wait for next week's regularly scheduled Federal Open Market 
Committee meeting. 

     The Fed set aside its inflation concerns for the time being and 
slashed the short-term rates it controls by three quarters of a 
percentage point. More easing seems sure to come -- and soon. 

     With only a week before its first meeting of 2008 was set to begin, 
the FOMC slashed the key federal funds rate 75 basis points to 3.5% 
following an emergency conference call. The Board of Governors, the core 
of the FOMC, cut the discount rate a like amount to 4%. 

     It was the FOMC's first intermeeting rate cut since September 17, 
2001, after the 9/11 terrorist attacks. That year had begun with an 
intermeeting cut on Jan. 3 amid growing worries about recession and 
deflation risks. 

     The 75 basis point rate cut, which took the federal funds rate to 
its lowest level since August 2005, was the largest the FOMC has made 
since October 1984 and is proportionately much bigger, since the FOMC at 
that time was cutting from 11.30%. 

     Notwithstanding the magnitude of this morning's easing move, few 
expect it to be the last. The only question in Fed watchers' minds is 
how much further the FOMC will cut the funds rate at the end of two days 
of meetings next Tuesday and Wednesday. Views differ. 

     Only St. Louis Federal Reserve Bank President William Poole 
dissented against the action, preferring to wait until next week. 

     U.S. Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson praised the Fed for 
demonstrating that it is "nimble and able to move quickly" and said the 
outsized easing move "should be a confidence builder." 

     In making a preemptive rate cut of this magnitude before the real 
economy is clearly in recession, the FOMC has temporarily deferred its 
concerns about elevated inflation. Poole, ironically, among other 
officials, had said recently that "contained" inflation expectations 
give the Fed the latitude to ease substantially if needed without much 
inflationary consequences. 

     In announcing the rate cuts, the Fed said the FOMC "took this 
action in view of a weakening of the economic outlook and increasing 
downside risks to growth." 

     "While strains in short-term funding markets have eased somewhat, 
broader financial market conditions have continued to deteriorate and 
credit has tightened further for some businesses and households," the 
statement continued. "Moreover, incoming information indicates a 
deepening of the housing contraction as well as some softening in labor 
markets." 

     The announcement added that "the Committee expects inflation to 
moderate in coming quarters, but it will be necessary to continue to 
monitor inflation developments carefully." 

     In a clear, implied policy bias toward further rate reductions, the 
Fed declared, "appreciable downside risks to growth remain." And it said 
the FOMC "will continue to assess the effects of financial and other 
developments on economic prospects and will act in a timely manner as 
needed to address those risks." 

     Leading up to the Fed action, there had been a series of weaker- 
than-expected economic numbers starting with a surprisingly weak 
December employment report and continuing with a decline in retail sales 
and flat industrial production. 

     But the real proximate cause of the Fed's dramatic rate cuts, 
undoubtedly, was financial, not economic. 

     On Monday, when U.S. markets were closed in observance of Martin 
Luther King day, foreign markets were validating the adage that when the 
United States sneezes, the world catches cold. 

     With recession talk mounting, investors' hopes dimmed for 
profitable exports to the huge U.S. market. Neither Fed Chairman Ben 
Bernanke's interest rate cut signals nor President Bush's proposed $145 
billion fiscal stimulus plan were enough to support stocks around the 
world. 

     Investors worldwide have become increasingly risk averse, both 
because of uncertainty over what new subprime mortgage related losses 
might be revealed in the financial sector and because of fear that 
forthcoming corporate earnings reports will reflect a fall-off of demand 
for companies' goods and services. 

     Starting with a near 4% drop in Japan, the selling wave gained 
momentum as markets opened. Hong Kong's Hang Seng index plunged 5.5%; 
Indian stocks slid 7.4%. In Europe, stocks were down 6.8% in France, 
7.2% in Germany and 5.5% in London. 

     Early Tuesday, U.S. stock futures were down sharply ahead of the 
opening bell, and cash was flooding into safe haven Treasury securities, 
depressing their yields. 

      Bank of America chief economist Mickey Levy said that in acting as 
it did, the FOMC "clearly acknowledged that it was behind the curve in 
easing." And he said the Fed "knows its credibility would be damaged if 
the economy slipped into recession." 

     Levy predicted the Fed "will continue to ease," but said he expects 
only a 25 basis point rate cut at next week's meeting because the Fed 
has already "eased aggressively." 

     "The problem we run into is that the problems facing the economy 
and the financial system are centered around housing and the subprime 
mortgage market," Levy continued. "The Fed can't resolve all of the 
economy's problems." 

     Besides, he added, "the real difficulty we face now is that 
monetary policy works with a lag." So the Fed's rate cuts will not take 
effect for awhile. And yet, he went on, "I've never seen a situation 
where market psychology has spiraled down so far relative to economic 
fundamentals." 

     Others are expecting continued aggressive easing next week. 
 Wachovia senior economist Mark Vitner said a 50 basis point rate cut 
next Wednesday afternoon is apt to be announced. 

     Vitner observed that there is "a disconnect" between the economy 
and financial markets. "The economy doesn't look nearly as sick as the 
financial markets." He noted that weekly jobless claims have been 
falling, for example. 

     Even so, "it has to be 50," Vitner said. "I really don't think you 
do 75 today and then say we can just do 25 next week unless we see a 
total reversal in global stock markets." 

     Vitner said the FOMC needs to "be definitive" and "take the real 
federal funds rate down into negative territory." 

     The Fed's attitude should be: We don't have a recession, but we're 
going to conduct monetary policy as if we have one," said Vitner. 

     Levy, a former colleague of Poole on the monetarist Shadow Open 
Market Committee, said the FOMC need not worry about inflation for the 
time being. 

     "Core inflation is going to decelerate in response to slower 
nominal spending growth and weaker economic activity," Levy projected. 
"Eventually this Fed easing will generate stronger nominal spending 
growth, but only with a lag after that will it generate higher 
inflation." 

     "Eventually some of (the easing) will have to be taken away," said 
Levy, "but inflation is not a problem." 

     In a Jan. 15 article, Market News International reported that "an 
'exceptionally alert' group of Federal Reserve policymakers could 
conceivably act preemptively at almost any time in an effort to provide 
for economic and financial stability, depending on the stimuli."

 HDFed Cuts Rates Aggressively, Sets Stage for More Next Week      WC1,242 words   PD22 January 2008   ET16:16   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD

By Steven K. Beckner
               
     Market News International - In the wake of a global market collapse 
that portended further heavy losses of equity wealth in the United 
States, the Federal Reserve chose to act immediately and aggressively 
before U.S. markets opened Tuesday morning to protect the economy, 
rather than wait for next week's regularly scheduled Federal Open Market 
Committee meeting. 

     The Fed set aside its inflation concerns for the time being and 
slashed the short-term rates it controls by three quarters of a 
percentage point. More easing seems sure to come -- and soon. 

     With only a week before its first meeting of 2008 was set to begin, 
the FOMC slashed the key federal funds rate 75 basis points to 3.5% 
following an emergency conference call. The Board of Governors, the core 
of the FOMC, cut the discount rate a like amount to 4%. 

     It was the FOMC's first intermeeting rate cut since September 17, 
2001, after the 9/11 terrorist attacks. That year had begun with an 
intermeeting cut on Jan. 3 amid growing worries about recession and 
deflation risks. 

     The 75 basis point rate cut, which took the federal funds rate to 
its lowest level since August 2005, was the largest the FOMC has made 
since October 1984 and is proportionately much bigger, since the FOMC at 
that time was cutting from 11.30%. 

     Notwithstanding the magnitude of this morning's easing move, few 
expect it to be the last. The only question in Fed watchers' minds is 
how much further the FOMC will cut the funds rate at the end of two days 
of meetings next Tuesday and Wednesday. Views differ. 

     Only St. Louis Federal Reserve Bank President William Poole 
dissented against the action, preferring to wait until next week. 

     U.S. Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson praised the Fed for 
demonstrating that it is "nimble and able to move quickly" and said the 
outsized easing move "should be a confidence builder." 

     In making a preemptive rate cut of this magnitude before the real 
economy is clearly in recession, the FOMC has temporarily deferred its 
concerns about elevated inflation. Poole, ironically, among other 
officials, had said recently that "contained" inflation expectations 
give the Fed the latitude to ease substantially if needed without much 
inflationary consequences. 

     In announcing the rate cuts, the Fed said the FOMC "took this 
action in view of a weakening of the economic outlook and increasing 
downside risks to growth." 

     "While strains in short-term funding markets have eased somewhat, 
broader financial market conditions have continued to deteriorate and 
credit has tightened further for some businesses and households," the 
statement continued. "Moreover, incoming information indicates a 
deepening of the housing contraction as well as some softening in labor 
markets." 

     The announcement added that "the Committee expects inflation to 
moderate in coming quarters, but it will be necessary to continue to 
monitor inflation developments carefully." 

     In a clear, implied policy bias toward further rate reductions, the 
Fed declared, "appreciable downside risks to growth remain." And it said 
the FOMC "will continue to assess the effects of financial and other 
developments on economic prospects and will act in a timely manner as 
needed to address those risks." 

     Leading up to the Fed action, there had been a series of weaker- 
than-expected economic numbers starting with a surprisingly weak 
December employment report and continuing with a decline in retail sales 
and flat industrial production. 

     But the real proximate cause of the Fed's dramatic rate cuts, 
undoubtedly, was financial, not economic. 

     On Monday, when U.S. markets were closed in observance of Martin 
Luther King day, foreign markets were validating the adage that when the 
United States sneezes, the world catches cold. 

     With recession talk mounting, investors' hopes dimmed for 
profitable exports to the huge U.S. market. Neither Fed Chairman Ben 
Bernanke's interest rate cut signals nor President Bush's proposed $145 
billion fiscal stimulus plan were enough to support stocks around the 
world. 

     Investors worldwide have become increasingly risk averse, both 
because of uncertainty over what new subprime mortgage related losses 
might be revealed in the financial sector and because of fear that 
forthcoming corporate earnings reports will reflect a fall-off of demand 
for companies' goods and services. 

     Starting with a near 4% drop in Japan, the selling wave gained 
momentum as markets opened. Hong Kong's Hang Seng index plunged 5.5%; 
Indian stocks slid 7.4%. In Europe, stocks were down 6.8% in France, 
7.2% in Germany and 5.5% in London. 

     Early Tuesday, U.S. stock futures were down sharply ahead of the 
opening bell, and cash was flooding into safe haven Treasury securities, 
depressing their yields. 

      Bank of America chief economist Mickey Levy said that in acting as 
it did, the FOMC "clearly acknowledged that it was behind the curve in 
easing." And he said the Fed "knows its credibility would be damaged if 
the economy slipped into recession." 

     Levy predicted the Fed "will continue to ease," but said he expects 
only a 25 basis point rate cut at next week's meeting because the Fed 
has already "eased aggressively." 

     "The problem we run into is that the problems facing the economy 
and the financial system are centered around housing and the subprime 
mortgage market," Levy continued. "The Fed can't resolve all of the 
economy's problems." 

     Besides, he added, "the real difficulty we face now is that 
monetary policy works with a lag." So the Fed's rate cuts will not take 
effect for awhile. And yet, he went on, "I've never seen a situation 
where market psychology has spiraled down so far relative to economic 
fundamentals." 

     Others are expecting continued aggressive easing next week. 
 Wachovia senior economist Mark Vitner said a 50 basis point rate cut 
next Wednesday afternoon is apt to be announced. 

     Vitner observed that there is "a disconnect" between the economy 
and financial markets. "The economy doesn't look nearly as sick as the 
financial markets." He noted that weekly jobless claims have been 
falling, for example. 

     Even so, "it has to be 50," Vitner said. "I really don't think you 
do 75 today and then say we can just do 25 next week unless we see a 
total reversal in global stock markets." 

     Vitner said the FOMC needs to "be definitive" and "take the real 
federal funds rate down into negative territory." 

     The Fed's attitude should be: We don't have a recession, but we're 
going to conduct monetary policy as if we have one," said Vitner. 

     Levy, a former colleague of Poole on the monetarist Shadow Open 
Market Committee, said the FOMC need not worry about inflation for the 
time being.

nominal spending growth and weaker economic activity," Levy projected.

growth, but only with a lag after that will it generate higher

     "Eventually some of (the easing) will have to be taken away," said 
Levy, "but inflation is not a problem." 

     In a Jan. 15 article, Market News International reported that "an 
'exceptionally alert' group of Federal Reserve policymakers could 
conceivably act preemptively at almost any time in an effort to provide 
for economic and financial stability, depending on the stimuli."

   NSe11 : Economic Performance/Indicators | e12 : Economic/Monetary Policy | e1202 : Interest Rates | e1101 : Economic Growth | ecat : Economic News   REusa : United States | namz : North America   PUBMarket News International, Inc.   ANDocument MARNEW0020080122e41m000by  



 SECOMMENT   HDChoppy waters lie ahead after the Fed's move.    BYBy MOHAMED EL-ERIAN    WC708 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDAsia Ed1   PGPage 9   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPRather than wait for its meeting next week, the US Federal Reserve implemented an emergency cut of 75 basis points yesterday. This took its key interest rate, the federal funds rate, down to 3.5 per cent compared with 5.25 per cent just six months ago. 

Yet what would have been interpreted just a few days ago as a bold policy measure to get ahead of the curve is now seen by some as an insufficient attempt to play catch-up with a rapidly deteriorating situation. 
   TDThe immediate market reaction to the Fed announcement points to continued scepticism as to whether the policymakers fully grasp the severity of the position. The interest rate markets are pricing in more cuts next week and have lowered the rate at which they expect the current interest rate cycle to bottom out. 

Meanwhile, rather than equity market sentiment being turned around decisively by the move, investors now understand better what interest rates and credit markets have been signalling for a while: that the continuing damage to the financial system is being embedded more deeply into the chain of economic relationships, increasing the overall default risk in the economy. 

The recent troubles of the bond insurers serve only to reinforce the image of falling dominos now that the major banks have taken important write-offs. 

As long as this market mentality persists, the fresh capital currently on the sideline will only engage forcefully and sustainably in risk markets if valuations become excessively cheap. 

Meanwhile, the Fed will be restoring liquidity to the parts of the system that are closest to it, accentuating the divergence that we have witnessed recently between a normalising interbank market (highlighted by the moves in the London inter-bank offered rate) and unstable and volatile credit and equity markets. 

In this context, and in the absence of belief that a meaningful fiscal response will accompany the monetary policy loosening, valuations of stocks and other assets rather than corrective government actions will act as the main driving factor in "clearing" markets to enable them to stabilise and function smoothly again. 

Prices will have to get low enough to skew the balance of risk significantly to the advantage of new investors to tempt them in. Otherwise, they will simply wait on the sidelines. 

Recent developments will also accentuate the divergence between transparent segments of the markets and structured finance, where there are still fundamental concerns about methods of valuation. Liquidity and cash will remain king. 

Can other countries "decouple" from the problems of the US? Here, it is important to distinguish between market valuations and economic trends. An increase in the overall level of risk aversion around the world makes it hard for markets to decouple. Economic decoupling is a greater possibility, supported by underlying productivity trends and the larger degree of policy flexibility in some other key countries. 

All this points to a particularly choppy time for markets. In running their portfolios, investors will have to factor in sharp changes in relative valuations between asset classes and need to manage cash carefully. Risk mitigation trades, implemented during the complacent market era that ended last summer, will prove particularly effective. 

Over the longer term, markets will be dealing with a tricky issue of timing: how to reconcile the effects of delayed policy responses with what will eventually be a turn upwards in valuation as fresh capital is invested. 

It is to be hoped, before this happens, that we will see meaningful progress in addressing an important issue that lies at the heart of both the era of excessive complacency and the subsequent period of major correction: it is time for the market and policy infrastructure to catch up with the new activities that have been enabled by the realignment of global economic power and the technical innovations in the financial industry. 

The longer such progress is delayed, the greater the probability of further damage as a result of the consequences of lax risk management and less potent policy instruments. 

The writer is co-chief executive and co-chief investment officer of Pimco. He was previously president and chief executive officer of Harvard Management Company

 SEMARKETS &INVESTING   HDPrice of gold rallies amid other gains.    BYBy JAVIER BLAS    WC580 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDAsia Ed1   PGPage 22   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPGold prices rallied and other commodities posted small gains after the US Federal Reserve cut interest rates in an effort to prop up the weakening US economy. 

Spot bullion in London jumped to Dollars 894.3 a troy ounce as the US dollar weakened against the euro after the Fed cut its main interest rate by a hefty 75 basis points to 3.5 per cent and said it was ready to take further action. 
   TDAnita Soni of Credit Suisse said: "Upward pressure on the price of gold is likely being driven by weakness of the US dollar fuelled by US rate cuts." 

Before the central bank's action, gold prices had tumbled to a three-week low of Dollars 849.50 an ounce as traders took profits from the recent rally in precious metals. 

Earlier this month, gold prices hit an all-time high of Dollars 914 an ounce. 

Yesterday's price jump was also boosted by positive comments from investment banks Morgan Stanley and Credit Suisse which raised their forecasts for average gold prices in 2008 to Dollars 950 an ounce and above Dollars 1,000 an ounce in 2009. 

Barclays Capital reiterated its positive view towards the metal, forecasting a test of the Dollars 1,000 an ounce before the summer. 

Suki Cooper, a precious metals analyst at Barclays Capital in London, said: "A significant number of positive external drivers such as a weak dollar, inflation concerns and slower US growth are set to support prices" 

UBS, on the other hand, remained cautious and reiterated its one-month target of Dollars 850 an ounce for gold on the weakness of physical jewellery demand. 

Other analysts also continue to be guarded as exchange-traded funds, the biggest driver of investment gold demand in recent years, last week suffered surprisingly large outflows. 

Other commodity prices pared losses or moved to positive territory after heavy falls earlier yesterday triggered by fears that the US economic slowdown could spread to emerging economies such as China and India, the main engines of fresh demand for raw materials. 

But traders remained cautious and warned that base metals and energy commodities could come under further pressure as soon as the interest rate cut euphoria fades away. 

Opec offered the starkest warning of the dangers lying ahead, saying in its monthly report: "With mounting evidence of a slowdown in US economic growth . . . fears of a downright recession have multiplied." 

Sales of commodities, particularly of agricultural raw materials, aimed at raising funds - to offset losses in others markets - also contributed to the price fall in early morning, traders said. 

Robert Laughlin of MF Global in London said that the immediate risk for commodities was "a liquidation of open positions by some market participants in order to finance positions on other markets." 

Crude oil prices rose on the day, reversing a fall to the lowest level in six weeks before the Fed's interest rate cut. 

In late trading in London, ICE March Brent rose 29 cents to Dollars 87.76 a barrel. Earlier, it fell to a low of Dollars 85.00 a barrel. In New York, Nymex February WestTexas Intermediate moved to Dollars 89.23 a barrel, a drop of Dollars 1.12 from Friday's close. 

On the London Metal Exchange base metals recovered from early losses. Copper rose 1.9 per cent to Dollars 7,010 per tonne.

 SEGLOBAL MARKETS TURMOIL   HDTreasury yields fall on Fed cuts.    BYBy JOANNA CHUNG and MICHAEL MACKENZIE    WC362 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDEurope Ed1   PGPage 27   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPUS Treasury yields fell sharply on expectations that the US Federal Reserve would follow yesterday's unusual 75 basis point interest rate cut with further easing next week. 

The sharp falls in equity markets around the world - and declines in US markets even after the Fed's move - meant investors were continuing to prize the safety of government debt amid worries of a US recession. 
   TDThe Fed cut its overnight rate to 3.50 per cent before markets reopened yesterday. It was its first inter-meeting cut since September 2001 and the biggest inter-meeting move in history. 

Government bond markets in the US and Europe have made sharp gains since last August, when credit and mortgage concerns alerted investors that the US economy was facing a tough 2008. 

The Fed holds its official January meeting next week, and in a statement yesterday said that "appreciable downside risks to growth remain," which bond traders believe keeps the door open for more cuts. Yesterday, interest rate futures priced in at least another 25bp and possibly a 50bp ease. 

"Since the Fed will not have enough time to see the impact on the real economy of this 75 bp of stimulus, it will only have a chance to take a look at the response of the market, potentially easing more if the market demands it, which we think it will," said Ian Lyngen, bond strategist at RBS Greenwich Capital. 

The yield on the interest-rate sensitive two-year US Treasury note was at 2.11 per cent yesterday and had reached a low of 1.99 per cent, its lowest level since April 2004. In July, the note yielded about 5 per cent. It began the year at 3.06 per cent, well below the prevailing Fed funds rate. 

But any sign of equities stabilising will likely push yields back up. Charles Diebel, fixed income strategist at Nomura, said: "There can be little doubt that bonds are well over-extended here . . . However, for the moment this is a fear-driven market rather than a value- driven one."  
   RF20080123E127.072   INi81401 : Central Banking | ibnk : Banking/Credit | i814 : Banking   NSe1202 : Interest Rates | mcat : Commodity/Financial Market News | e11 : Economic Performance/Indicators | e212 : Government Borrowing | ecat : Economic News | gpol : Domestic Politics | e12 : Economic/Monetary Policy | e21 : Government Finance | gcat : Political/General News | gpir : Politics/International Relations | ncat : Content Types | nfact : Factiva Filters | nfce : FC&E Exclusion Filter   REusa : United States | namz : North America   PUBThe Financial Times Limited (AAIW/EIW)   ANDocument FTFT000020080123e41n00021  



 SELEX COLUMN   HDSlash and Bernanke.    WC336 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDLondon Ed2   PGPage 18   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPBen Bernanke had a holiday weekend to watch the meltdown in global equity markets and to panic about how the storm would hit the US. Rather than wait, the Federal Reserve chairman acted pre-emptively, bringing forward the aggressive 75 basis point interest rate cut that would otherwise have arrived next week. 

The biggest inter-meeting rate cut in Fed history served as a circuit breaker against the threat of a vicious cycle down in the markets. For now. 
   TDOver time, sharply lower rates will help the US economy. A steeper yield curve should feed through into better bank profits over time and allow them to rebuild capital. More immediately, lower rates will help reduce the payment shock from mortgage resets and bolster confidence in general. 

But the shock cut is no solution in itself. US house prices still need to fall further. Credit markets must continue working off the huge excesses of recent years. Both will take time. Meanwhile, the second round effects - weaker consumer spending and rising bad debts for the banking sector - are only just starting to show through. And equity investors seem to have woken up only belatedly to the fact that profits could fall materially. 

Just as the Fed struggled to make its interest rate rises felt between 2003 and 2006, as financial institutions got looser and more creative with their lending, it will struggle to ensure aggressive cuts are transmitted through to corporate and consumer borrowers as lenders draw in their horns. Interest rate cuts have their biggest impact with a lag of about a year. In this case financial institutions are already battered and bruised by the credit market meltdown. Lending is likely to continue being curtailed, even as rates fall. 

Given the wave of bad economic and market data, the Fed will probably slash rates by another half point next week. But it has now fired its big cannon. As the Fed's armoury becomes depleted, the financial instability will continue.

 HDBank of Canada lowers key  interest rate  by 25 bps to 4.0%      WC363 words   PD22 January 2008   ET15:09   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD
--- As U.S. Federal reserve cuts by 75 bps, BoC sticks with -0.25% but 
says further cut likely "in the near term"

By Courtney Tower

     OTTAWA (MNI)- In the face of plunging world financial markets and 
while the U.S. Federal reserve in an unscheduled move slashed its key
rate by 75 basis points, the  Bank of Canada today cut its policy rate 
by a third of that, 25 basis points, to 4.0%.

    The Bank rejected several current financial economists' calls for 
a 50 basis points reduction, given the deteriorating world conditions,
but signalled another cut shortly, on or before the next scheduled rate
setting March 4.

     In its scheduled statement, lowering the rate to 4.0% from the 4.25 
set in December, the Bank said that in line with a deteriorated 
outlook "further monetary stimulus is likely to be required in the near 
term to keep aggregate supply and demand in balance and to return 
inflation to target over the medium term."

     Core and total inflation, at 1.7% and 2.7% at the latest reading, 
are both expected to fall below 1.5% by mid-2008 before returning to the 
2% target by the end of 2009, the Bank said.

     The 2008 outlook for the United States economy is "significantly 
weaker" than the  +2.1% growth the Bank had expected for the U.S. last 
October, the Bank said. The U.S. picture had been dragged down by 
deteriorated financial market conditions, tighter credit in industrial 
countries, "and a deeper, more prolonged decline in the U.S. 
residential housing sector."

     The weaker U.S. outlook "will lead to additional downward pressure 
on export growth" for Canada, the Bank said.
     
     "However, despite tighter credit conditions, domestic demand in 
Canada is projected to remain strong." This strength would be supported 
"by continued income growth associated with the increase in commodity 
prices....."

     "Overall, the Bank now projects weaker growth in 2008 than was 
expected in October, with the economy moving into modest excess supply 
in the second quarter of this year," the Bank said.

 SEWORLD NEWS - EMERGENCY RATE CUT   HDPressure on ECB to soften tough line.    BYBy RALPH ATKINS    WC589 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDAsia Ed1   PGPage 2   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPThe steep cut in US interest rates intensified the pressure on the European Central Bank to soften its hardline stance and pave the way for eurozone borrowing costs to fall as well. 

The ECB dislikes any suggestion of being pushed around by financial markets or politicians. But it is aware that the economic outlook for the 15-country eurozone could change in the light of US events and that its reaction might have a direct impact on market nerves. 
   TDFinancial markets yesterday fully priced in a quarter percentage point cut in ECB rates to 3.75 per cent by May. "The pressure for a careful and timely recalibration of their rhetoric has clearly increased," said Marco Annunziata, chief economist of Unicredit.

The ECB's focus, in public at least, will remain on inflation - which at 3.1 per cent remains far beyond its target range of an annual rate "below but close" to 2 per cent. But ECB president Jean-Claude Trichet's warning less than two weeks ago - when he said "pre-emptive" interest rate rises were possible to head off inflationary threats posed by high wage settlements - appears overtaken by events. 

If nothing else, the ECB could argue that the heightened economic gloom and financial turmoil will help curb wage demands. 

Amid signs of differing opinion among the members of the ECB's 21-strong governing council, the central bank's message had started to moderate even before yesterday's Federal Reserve move, with several making clear that eurozone growth forecasts were rapidly becoming over-optimistic. 

While the eurozone has not seen a general housing market collapse and has largely escaped direct macroeconomic consequences of the credit squeeze, the threat of a US recession is a serious concern. Tumbling equity prices, if sustained, would add to the risks to the real economy by threatening business confidence, for instance, even if the impact would not be as great as in the US or UK. 

In judging the impact on inflation, the ECB will want to avoid drawing hasty conclusions. Some in Frankfurt might fear the Fed overreacted yesterday. For now, eurozone economic fundamentals remain sound in the ECB's view, especially with unemployment falling - unlike in the US. Jurgen Stark, ECB executive board member responsible for economic analysis, warned against "angst and panic" in an interview with Die Zeit, the German newspaper, released last night, adding that eurozone growth could remain in line with its long-term trend. 

What is likely to be crucial is the extent of the US slowdown. If it can notch up positive growth, albeit it at a significantly slower pace, the idea the eurozone could grow this year at a rate approaching 2 per cent (after 2.6 per cent in 2007) would not seem outlandish. But a full-blown US recession, coupled with sustained financial market turmoil, would drag eurozone growth lower. 

Ken Wattret of BNP Paribas said that in early 2001 the ECB had also taken a relatively optimistic stance on the eurozone's ability to shake off problems from across the Atlantic. "In the end, the spillover from the recession in the US, problems in equity markets and the recognition that the eurozone economy was in trouble saw the ECB capitulate, despite uncomfortable inflation and a low euro exchange rate." 

In May 2001 the ECB cut its main interest rate by a quarter point, initiating an easing cycle not reversed until December 2005.

 HDECB Constancio: US Slowdown Or Recession Will Hit Europe      WC226 words   PD22 January 2008   ET14:48   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD

     LISBON (MNI) - The chances of a recession in the US have risen and 
it will have an impact on the European economy, European Central Bank 
Governing Council member Vitor Constancio said Tuesday. 

     "The probability has risen without doubt. It doesn't mean it will 
happen, but the probability has risen, which means that the slowdown in 
the US will be sharper than predicted," Constancio told reporters after 
a closed conference here. 

     "And this will have consequences also for the European economy," he 
said. 

     Constancio's comments came just moments before the US Federal 
Reserve announced a surprise inter-meeting interest rate cut of 75 basis 
points, citing the darkening domestic economic outlook. 

     Constancio said eurozone growth this year would be lower, though he 
wouldn't say how much lower. "It's early to make a revision, but it is 
evident that the risk that growth will be a bit lower, just a little 
lower, has risen," he said. 

     Constancio also said there is no pre-determined path for ECB 
interest rate moves. "What we will do depends really on the information 
in real time that we analyze at our monthly meetings," he said. "There 
is no pre-announcement trajectory for interest rates by the ECB and the 
Governing Council."

 HDECB Update: Fed Cut Ups Pressure;Unclear How Long ECB To Wait      WC1,347 words   PD22 January 2008   ET18:26   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD
-- Regular Brief Analysis Of News And Events Pertinent To ECB Policy
-- See "ECB State Of Play" For Full Analysis Of ECB Policy Direction

     FRANKFURT, Jan. 22 (MNI) - Tuesday's massive 75 basis point 
interest rate cut by the US Federal Reserve in the face of a global 
stock market meltdown will increase pressure on the European Central 
Bank to jettison its tightening bias and consider the need for cutting 
its own rates in response to intensifying threats to economic growth. 

     It is unclear how long the ECB can resist that pressure or how 
difficult it might be for the Governing Council to achieve a quick 
consensus on the possible need to shift its monetary policy into 
reverse. Some Council members apparently still see very little urgency, 
judging by their comments this week on inflation risks and economic 
growth near potential. 

     It is likely the ECB will be content for now to let the Fed do the 
heavy lifting - as it should do in response to a homegrown problem. If 
confidence is shored up enough for markets to gain a foothold, it's 
possible the ECB would refrain from action while slowly altering its 
rhetoric to signal a shift to a neutral bias -- and possibly beyond 
neutral. 

     Arguing against a near-term rate cut is the ECB's deeply rooted 
mistrust of dramatic policy actions aimed at fine-tuning the economy. 
The Bundesbank's old "steady hand" approach is still the guiding ethos 
at the ECB. 

     On a more complicated note, the ECB might face a credibility 
problem that could take some time to resolve. If it continues to talk 
tough on inflation in the face of crumbling growth prospects, it could 
risk being perceived as blind to the threats facing the real economy and 
in danger of falling behind the curve. 

     The problem would not completely disappear even if the ECB did 
shift gears swiftly in response to a deteriorating economic and 
financial environment. Having virtually painting itself into a corner 
with its harsh inflation rhetoric, the ECB would then face unflattering 
questions after the fact about its prior reading of reality. 

     If push came to shove, however, any concerns it might have about 
projecting a credible and authoritative image would nonetheless not 
prevent the ECB from responding appropriately -- especially if it were 
convinced that a greater than expected slowdown would likely reduce 
inflation dangers. 

     The ECB would probably be quite happy not to have to react to a 
market meltdown at this point, as this would give it time to gradually 
shift its policy stance and thus keep unpleasant questions to a minimum. 

     That shift has already started, and the ECB has indicated that it 
is increasingly aware of the risk that the eurozone slowdown will be 
more pronounced than it had anticipated. It has also watered down its 
assertions that Europe is relatively insulated from U.S. economic 
developments, and the latest equity market upheaval should make it even 
more alert to the risks involved. 

     That said, the ECB will want to wait for more evidence that the 
real economy in Europe could be hit hard by the financial disruptions 
amid signs of slowing demand in other parts of the world. 

     It may still take some exceptionally bad eurozone economic data to 
dislodge the ECB from its position of "very close" monitoring. And if 
markets do stabilize from here on, that will reaffirm the view of some 
Council members that the situation is not dramatic. 

     If, however, equity markets and confidence in general continue to 
spiral downward, the ECB could be forced to cut rates more quickly than 
the markets have been expecting, its inflation worries notwithstanding. 
Europe's economic fundamentals may be sturdier than those of the U.S. at 
the moment, but they could quickly become shaky if spillover effects are 
protracted or intensify. 

     Barring a severe jolt to the economy, it is hard to see the ECB 
communicating a major shift in its policy stance before the next staff 
projections are published in early March. 

     A major downward revision to the projections for economic growth -- 
which would tend to lower those for inflation as well -- could be the 
trigger for a move to neutral or possibly even to an easing bias. 
Conceivably, this could open the way to an ECB rate cut as soon as Q2. 

-- 

     FRANKFURT, Jan. 21 (MNI) - The weekend brought more evidence of a 
shift in ECB thinking towards the view that hiking interest rates is no 
longer realistic in the face of weakening economic growth, heightened 
uncertainty and expectations that inflation will recede. 

     Comments last week by ECB Governing Council member Yves Mersch on 
mounting risks to activity with possible dampening effects on inflation 
got the ball rolling. The financial markets may have gone overboard in 
their initial conclusion that Mersch was signalling a near-term rate 
cut, and that assessment has since been gently dispelled by the central 
bank. 

     However, the view is increasingly taking hold that the ECB won't 
hike rates again and that it is only a matter of time before its 
tightening bias is cast aside. In fact, financial markets are now fully 
pricing in an ECB rate cut by July.   

     Two more Council members, one a putative policy hawk, the other a 
nominal dove, made the latest remarks to bolster the growing impression 
in the markets that the next ECB adjustment will be a rate cut, even if 
the move may still be some time away. 

     Nout Wellink, the Dutch central banker seen as one of the Council's 
more influential hawks, said in an interview that economic growth in the 
eurozone this year will likely fall short of what the ECB expects. 

     Wellink noted that the latest Eurosystem staff projections call for 
GDP growth this year of between 1.5% and 2.5%. The ECB has repeatedly 
said that it expects growth to remain close to trend potential near 2%. 

     "But the world is not so exactly predictable," Wellink said. "I 
think it [2008 growth] will sooner wind up at 1.5% than 2.5%." 

     Wellink attributed the slowdown to the "crisis of confidence" 
caused by the fallout from U.S. subprime mortgage crisis. He also poured 
cold water on the idea that Europe can de-couple from U.S. developments. 

     In a separate interview, Guy Quaden of Belgium, considered a dove, 
played down worries about inflation, stressing that the current spike is 
apt to be a temporary phenomenon and should not be overstated. 

     According to Quaden, the inflation surge should be "temporary" and 
a "hump rather than a move towards a new [higher] level." Inflation 
should abate in the second half and return to levels below 2% in 2009, 
he said. 

     While the ECB is "certainly not satisfied" with elevated price 
pressures, "we should not dramatize this affair," Quaden added. He went 
on to say that the ECB does not react mechanically to current inflation 
rates and must focus on the medium-term outlook -- yet another hint that 
an inflation rate above 3% at present need not prompt an interest rate 
hike. 

     Importantly, however, Quaden did not give an all-clear on price 
risks, pointing to Germany's upcoming wage round. If the moderation of 
past years "disappears, then the ECB obviously has a problem," he 
warned. He added that the central bank has "no certainties" that its 
baseline scenario of inflation falling below 2% next year will be borne 
out. 

     As long as this remains the case, markets can continue to expect 
occasional tough talk from the ECB on the risk of second-round inflation 
effects and promises of action should they emerge. However, it has 
become less likely that those threats would be acted on. 

     The ECB is in the process of downgrading its policy position. As 
with any process, it may take some time before the ECB communicates that 
it has moved to "neutral," let alone to an easing bias. 

--Frankfurt bureau, 00-49-69-720-142; Email: christian@marketnews.com

 HDMORTGAGES/AGENCIES-Robust Fed rate cut buoys mortgage bonds   WC573 words   PD22 January 2008   ET22:13   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPBy Julie Haviv

NEW YORK, Jan 22 (Reuters ) - U.S. mortgage-backed securities outperformed rallying Treasuries on Tuesday as an unexpected and aggressive interest rate cut from the Federal Reserve brought buyers to the market.
   TDThe Federal Reserve conducted its biggest interest rate cut in over two decades, slashing its key federal funds target rate by three-quarters of a percentage point to 3.50 percent, the lowest level since September 2005.

The U.S. central bank's action was intended to ease market worries about dramatic losses the day before in global equity markets linked to fears of a spreading U.S. recession and tight credit.

U.S. Treasuries surged after the emergency rate cut but had limited success in staunching a sell-off in stocks.

For most MBS market participants, the action by the Federal Reserve was welcome news.

"The yield curve steepened sharply today, which is typically good for mortgage bonds because it boosts the bid for CMOs and improves funding levels," said an MBS trader on Wall Street.

"Convexity buying is also playing a big role today, mainly from servicers, and that is mostly benefiting lower coupons," he said.

The yield premium on Fannie Mae MBS paying 5.00 percent interest versus the benchmark 10-year Treasury note <US10YT=RR> narrowed to 1.37 percentage points on Tuesday from 1.421 points on Friday, according to Reuters data.

Active 15- and 30-year mortgage securities were 8/32 to 28/32 higher. Bond equivalent yields on 30-year 5.50 percent coupon MBS ranged from 4.779 percent to 4.840 percent.

Prices on 30-year 6 percent coupons were 11/32 higher. Bond equivalent yields ranged from 4.550 to 4.590 percent.

The steeper yield curve could increase bank demand for MBS and boost the new issue collateralized mortgage obligation market, both of which benefit mortgage bonds.

Collateralized mortgage obligations, or CMOs, are structured with mortgage-backed securities and tailored to meet specific requirements of investors.

A steeper yield curve is good for banks as they borrow short-term debt and lend long-term debt.

Agency debt securities mostly moved in sympathy with interest rate swap spreads, a proxy for investor risk appetite.

Fannie Mae 5.375 percent 10-year benchmark notes due June 12, 2017, were 1 basis point tighter at 55 basis points bid and 53 basis points offered over Treasuries.

U.S. 10-year Treasury notes were 1-10/32 higher at 106-1/32 to yield 3.481 percent. Yields, which move inversely to price, were down from Friday's 3.632 percent.

When rates head lower, participants in the mortgage market, particularly servicers, usually need to hedge by buying Treasuries, receiving in swaps or by adding MBS. This trade, called convexity hedging, could pick up if rates continue to fall.

A refinancing wave is very much a concern among MBS market participants right now.

Treasury yields, which are linked to mortgage rates, have plunged in recent weeks, which is already spurring a sharp increase in refinancing activity. This should cause prepayment speeds to surge higher over the coming months.

Prepayment speeds are key factors to determine the value of mortgage bonds. If prepayments rise or fall too quickly, they hurt the returns on mortgage securities. (Editing by James Dalgleish)

 HDTop Stories in the US, Europe and Asia Tuesday, Jan 22      WC1,459 words   PD22 January 2008   ET22:46   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD

*    Treasuries rallied Tuesday: On a 1:00 p.m. Friday to 3:00 pm ET 
Tuesday basis close, the cash 2-year note yield declined 28.3 bps to 
2.059%. The 5-year yield fell 22.7 bps to 2.623%. The 10-year yield fell 
16.6 bps to 3.483%, and the 30-year yield fell 7.1 bps to to 4.228%. The 
2-year/5-year curve steepened 5.6 bps to +56.4 bps, the 2-year/10-year 
also steepened 11.7 bps to +142.4 bps, and the 2-year/30-year steepened 
21.2 bps to +216.9 bps. 

*    The dollar was holding a mixed tone at the end of a tumultuous 
trading day Tuesday, nursing steep losses vs. the euro but holding 
slight gains vs. the yen as the greenback was undermined by a surprise, 
steep cut in official U.S. interest rates. Euro-dollar was trading at 
$1.4615 late in the Tuesday session, up from the $1.4500 level that 
marked the start of U.S. trading Tuesday. On the day, the euro traversed 
a broad $1.4485/1.4645 range. Dollar-yen meantime was trading at Y106.42 
in afternoon trading, up slightly from the Y106.12 marked at the start 
of the day and about the middle of the Y106.05/107.05 U.S. hours range. 

*    US Stocks: The Dow Jones Industrial Average closed down 1.06% at 
11,971 and the Nasdaq Composite down 2.04% at 2,292. 

*    In the wake of a global market collapse that portended further 
heavy losses of equity wealth in the United States, the Federal Reserve 
chose to act immediately and aggressively before U.S. markets opened 
Tuesday morning to protect the economy, rather than wait for next week's 
regularly scheduled Federal Open Market Committee meeting. The Fed set 
aside its inflation concerns for the time being and slashed the 
short-term rates it controls by three quarters of a percentage point. 
More easing seems sure to come -- and soon. 

*    U.S. Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson Tuesday morning reaffirmed 
that the primary focus of his department is to get through the current 
period of financial and housing market upheaval with "as little impact 
on our economy as possible." In a question and answer session during the 
U.S. Chamber of Commerce's Policy Insider series, Paulson said his 
department is working quite proactively on what "policy measures should 
be taken," in conjunction with the president's working group on the 
financial markets. 

*    Some 13 of the chief economists at the 20 primary dealerships 
Tuesday said they still expect a 50 bps ease in the Fed funds target to 
3.00% on January 30 despite the early morning 75 bps intermeeting cut by 
the monetary policy authority. According to a Market News International 
survey taken after the surprise rate cut announcement, some 3 expect a 
more mild 25 bps cut next week. Yet, two called for steady policy at the 
end of the month. Two dealers were yet to respond, including  Merrill 
Lynch and  Citigroup. 

*    Congressional Budget Office director Peter Orszag will present 
the CBO's updated budget and economic report this week to the House and 
Senate Budget Committees and the report, perhaps  more than any other in 
recent CBO history, will be taken with a huge grain of salt, budget 
experts say. 

*    The director of the federal regulator for Fannie Mae and  Freddie 
Mac Tuesday said the Federal Reserve's surprise slash in short-term 
rates by three-quarters of a percentage point will help the 
deteriorating U.S. housing market. 

*    Fannie Mae and  Freddie Mac could report wider losses than 
expected in the fourth quarter, possibly up to $16 billion combined in 
write-downs, as the subprime mortgage market deteriorates further and 
erodes their portfolio holdings, according to analysts at  Credit Suisse. 

*    January is looking no better than December in more bad news for 
U.S. retailers which are preparing themselves for a year of flat 
results, according to Market News International's weekly industry survey 
released Tuesday. MNI's rolling retail-trade diffusion index edged 
seven-tenths higher to 35.1 in the four-week period ended Jan. 19, 
remaining well below 50, the breakeven level for the index and 
reflecting wide pessimism in the sector. 

*    The fourth-quarter earning season has opened with strength in the 
U.S. capital goods producers which are reporting healthy and improving 
rates of growth, as Market News International's weekly capital goods 
index released Tuesday jumped more than five points to 60.2 in the 
four-week period ended Jan. 18, well over the 50 level to indicate wide 
optimism. Total sales growth is probably on the high side at 8.3%, with 
the four-week average offering a better reading at 5.1%. 

*    In the face of plunging world financial markets and while the 
U.S. Federal reserve in an unscheduled move slashed its key rate by 75 
basis points, the  Bank of Canada today cut its policy rate by a third of 
that, 25 basis points, to 4.0%. 

Europe

*    [ECB Paramo: Price Risks On Upside; Must Avoid 2nd Rd Effects] 
Price risks in the eurozone are skewed to the upside, and it is 
particularly important that wage and price setters avoid unleashing 
second round inflation effects, European Central Bank Executive Board 
member Jose Manuel Gonzalez-Paramo said in a newspaper interview 
published Tuesday. In this context, Gonzalez-Paramo told the Portuguese 
business daily Diario Economico, the ECB is prepared to take preventive 
action if necessary to ensure price stability. 

*    [ECB Constancio: Europe Not Immune To What Happens In US Econ.] The
United States economy appears headed for a "severe" slowdown or a 
recession, and it is bound to affect Europe, which is not immune to 
economic developments across the Atlantic, European Central Bank 
Governing Council member Vitor Constancio said today. Constancio, 
speaking to reporters outside a conference here, noted the recent 
turbulence in world stock market and said investors appear to be 
perceiving a change in fundamentals. He made clear that the ECB will not 
respond to the latest round of turmoil with monetary policy. 

*    [CBI Ind Trends: Jan Orders Steady; Pricing Plans Up] Demand 
for UK manufactured goods remained firm in January, confounding 
pessimistic expectations, according to the latest survey by business 
body the CBI. The total orders balance was unchanged at 2% in January 
compared with December, with the sector apparently untouched by the 
economic slowdown. Pricing intentions remained elevated with the balance 
of manufacturers expecting to raise prices climbing back to 21% from 
15%. 

*    [CBI Lambert: 25 bps BOE Rate Cut Justified; Larger Cut Risky] 
The Bank of England's Monetary Policy Committee could justify a 25 basis 
point interest rate reduction but a larger cut would risk its 
credibility, former MPC member and now Director General of the CBI, 
Richard Lambert, said Tuesday. Lambert told reporters that a "steeper" 
cut than 25 basis points would take risks with the credibility and 
reputation of the Monetary Policy Committee.

*    [Germany EcoMin: No Reason To Cut 2008 GDP F'cast Below +1.7%] 
German Economics Minister Michael Glos said Tuesday there is no need to 
cut the government's GDP growth forecast for this year by more than 0.3 
percentage point from the current 2.0% projection. Glos was asked by 
reporters if the planned downward revision of the 2008 growth forecast 
to 1.7% was not overly optimistic in view of this week's stock market 
crash. "There's no need to change [our growth revision again]," the 
minister replied.

Asia

*    [BOJ Sees Slower Japan Growth, Cycle Intact] The Bank of Japan on 
Tuesday said in an update to its semi-annual Outlook that the mechanism 
to transfer high corporate profits to consumer spending is still intact 
despite a slower pace of economic growth.

*    [Japan Supermarket Sales Lower]  Sales at supermarkets in Japan 
open for at least one year fell 1.8% in December from a year earlier to 
Y1.39 trillion, down for the 24th consecutive month, an industry group 
said Tuesday. The fall compares with a 0.4% year-on-year slide in 
November.

*    [Ota: No Need For Stock Market Intervention] The Japanese 
government sees no need for now to intervene to halt a route on stock 
markets as the turmoil is stemming from the US, said Hiroko Ota, the 
minister for economic and fiscal policy. "Stock markets across the world 
are falling and it basically stems from the United States," Ota told 
reporters. "It is difficult at the moment to mull action by Japan alone. 
Instead, we should cooperate globally," she said.

 HDANALYSIS-Fed cut gives US stock bulls last best hope for bottom      BYBy Ellis Mnyandu and Burton Frierson      WC997 words   PD22 January 2008   ET22:31   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPNEW YORK, Jan 22 (Reuters) - The U.S. Federal Reserve's emergency interest rate cut on Tuesday could be the last and best hope for stockmarket bulls on Wall Street, after the worst start to a year on record.   

But the surprise rate cut could also bring an end to the huge rally in U.S. bonds.   
   TDAfter tumbles in stock markets across Asia and Europe on Monday when U.S. markets were closed for a holiday, Wall Street stocks looked headed for a plunge on Tuesday, but the Fed's surprise rate cut staunched the worst of the bleeding and raised hopes that U.S. selling may be overdone.   

Before the rate cut announcement U.S. Treasuries prices had soared, sending yields to their lowest in four years as investors dumped riskier assets, before giving back some of the price gains.   

"The downside risk from the lows of today are minimal as we build the base," said Marc Pado, market strategist and technical analyst at  Cantor Fitzgerald & Co. in San Francisco.   

"You've met the conditions for a selling climax and now you're getting a reversal. Compared to the damage that we've seen overseas, we're only down a fraction."   

Analysts who study stock prices and volume statistically said the stock market appeared to have now seen that cathartic selling that could help it build a base from its "oversold" condition.   

The Dow Jones industrial average was down more than 3.5 percent below the 12,000 level on opening Tuesday, before ending down only 1.02 percent at 11,975.42.   

Similarly, the benchmark S&P500 index fell 3.78 percent before recovering to end down only 1.1 percent at 1,310.62.   

And the Nasdaq slumped 4.40 percent before bouncing to end down 2.04 percent at 2,292.27.   

Even so, there is still a potential for more downside in stock prices and bond yields that could test investors' faith, analysts said.   

At Tuesday's open the Dow broke below the 12,000 level after it broke through support levels around 12,500 last week.   

According to the chartists, the 11,500-11,600 levels on the Dow are not out of question for the Dow if the market were to fall further.   

For the S&P 500 index, they said further downside could see the benchmark index finding support in the 1,220-1,200 range.   

According to Pado at  Cantor Fitzgerald, the S&P 500 drop to an intra-session low of 1,274.29 possibly marks a decisive level from which the broader market could mount a rebound, albeit gradually.   

"Why you see the market coming back is the fact that the market realizes we've had climactic selling. The Fed is on our side and so is the administration and the global central banks," he said.   

The Nasdaq briefly crossed into bear market threshold earlier on Tuesday, dropping more than 20 percent from its 52-week closing high of Oct 31, but the near-term picture had been helped by the Fed's inter-meeting rate cut, analysts said.   

"Investors have thrown in the towel and I think that really marks the end of the selling for now. I believe that we're very close to a bounce, but I don't think that necessarily will mark an ultimate low," said Bruce Zaro, chief technical strategist at Delta Global Advisors Inc in Plymouth, Massachusetts.   

BUOYANT BONDS   

In debt markets, there is a only little more room for bond prices to move higher, even though the Federal Reserve has stepped up the pace of its monetary easing campaign, analysts said.   

Bond prices generally benefit from Fed interest rate cuts but they often factor in such monetary policy easing well in advance, as has been the case with the run-up in Treasuries prices over the last seven months.   

Much of the recent bond market rally also has been a reaction to the deterioration in the stock market. This flight to "quality" left two-year yields well below the central bank's benchmark overnight federal funds rate.   

"Right now Treasuries are basically an anti-stocks trade," said Jack Adkins, director of quantitative research at Action Economics in Denver. "I think there is one more downleg in stocks, one more push lower in yields for Treasuries and then they both reverse."   

Two-year notes <US2YT=RR> were last yielding 2.07 percent. Earlier, yields fell as far as 1.99 percent, their lowest since early 2004.   

Adkins said two-year yields, the most sensitive to views on Fed policy, could make a run at 1.80 percent and may bottom out at 1.75 percent, based on an analysis of short-term intra day patterns and general market retracement tendencies.   

Benchmark 10-year yields <US10YT=RR> are likely to find a bottom between 3.25 and 3.35 percent relatively soon. Earlier, they fell as far as 3.43 percent, their lowest since 2003.   

Adkins said the timing could depend on the outcome of the Fed's interest rate meeting next Wednesday and the U.S. government's monthly employment report two days later.   

The bond market should also see a rise in longer term yields relative to shorter maturities, with this steepening of the yield curve being driven by Fed rate cuts which should help to revive economic growth and inflation.   

Yields on 10-year notes are now about 140 basis points more than rates on two-year Treasuries. This is likely to head up to 175 basis points, said Jeff Hlavacek, director of fixed income trading at  BNP Paribas in New York.   

"Technically the thing that really stands out clearest is the curve," said Hlavacek.

LONDON, Jan 22 (Reuters) - The U.S. Federal Reserve cut its main interest rate by three quarters of a percentage point on Jan. 22 to 3.5 percent. On the same day the  Bank of Canada lowered its key rate by a quarter of a percentage point to 4.0 percent.   
   TDAs a result of these moves the average rate set by central banks of the Group of Seven nations fell to 3.50 percent from 3.70 percent while the average

the G7 nations plus six other major banks -- fell to 4.43 percent from 4.52

 HDCORRECTED - ANALYSIS-US Fed cuts, stimulus package won't stem foreclosures      BYBy Joanne Morrison      WC693 words   PD23 January 2008   ET22:44   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Corrects in paragraph 7 Wall Street estimates that $165 billion prime mortgages are subject to reset and that $370 billion in subprime mortgages will reset in 2008)   

WASHINGTON, Jan 23 (Reuters) - For Main Street, interest rate cuts and a plan by the White House and Congress to pump $150 billion into the economy are unlikely to keep many Americans from losing their dream of homeownership to foreclosure.   
   TDWall Street was quick to applaud an unusually aggressive interest rate cut from the Federal Reserve on Tuesday, which came as President George W. Bush and lawmakers scrambled to assemble a package of tax rebates and other measures intended to stave off a recession.   

But industry experts say these steps won't necessarily translate into lower mortgage costs for some 2 million Americans with risky subprime home loans with rates that are scheduled to adjust sharply higher over the next year.   

For those who qualify for traditional 30-year fixed-rate mortgages, lower rates are now available thanks to recent Fed interest rate cuts. But many subprime borrowers have mortgages larger than what their properties are worth, and experts don't see home values rising any time soon.   

"Home prices, at best, are going to level off. So long as home prices are not rising, the reset problem exists," said Bill Hampell, chief economist at the Credit Union National Association in Washington.   

In addition, the rates on most of these subprime mortgages only adjust up and never down, regardless of interest rate conditions in the market.   

Over the next year about $165 billion in prime adjustable rate mortgages are subject to resets, along with the $370 billion in subprime mortgages subject to reset. With prices falling, those resets are expected to lead to a cascading wave of foreclosures.   

"The interest rate is only a relative part of their problem. They have too much mortgage and they basically were promised that the equity in their house would grow and that they could refinance," said John Taylor, president of the National Community Reinvestment Coalition.   

Many homeowners who financed their homes with subprime mortgages did so expecting the value of those properties would rise, enabling them to refinance out of their pricey mortgages and into loans with better terms.   

But the housing market turned down and it continues to slide.   

HOME PRICES FALLING   

Over the 12 months through November, prices on previously owned homes fell 3.3 percent, according to the National Association of Realtors. In Western areas of the United States where home prices rose sharply during the boom, prices tumbled nearly 7.0 percent.   

In addition, new home construction has slowed to just half of the pace it hit when it peaked in 2006, and experts say it will not pick up until record high inventories of unsold homes are pared back. For that to happen, prices may need to fall further.   

Fixed mortgage rates, which are tied to yields on long-term bonds that fluctuate on the outlook for the economy, have dropped, but the spread between 30-year mortgage rates and the benchmark 10-year Treasury note has widened.   

And for non-conforming fixed-rate mortgages, those in excess of $417,000 -- commonly known as jumbo mortgages -- the spread is even wider.   

But tighter credit, brought on by a rise in foreclosures, has not led to better rates for riskier borrowers who do not qualify for traditional fixed-rate loans.   

"Rate cuts don't change the risk profile of borrowers out there," cautioned Bill Dunkelberg, chief economist at the National Federation of Independent Business.   

What's worse is that if the economy slows, and consumers lose their jobs, that could add up to more foreclosures, outside of the subprime arena, cautioned Hampell.   

"A recession would add on top of that some additional foreclosures and that's not (a scenario) that we've been factoring in so far," he said. (Reporting By Joanne Morrison; Editing by Clive McKeef)

 SELEADER   HDSharp Fed rate cuts are justified But US central bank must mitigate the risks they create.    WC482 words   PD23 January 2008   SNFinancial Times   SCFTFT   EDAsia Ed1   PGPage 8   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved 
   LPIt is hard to decide which words are more worrying: "You have a life-threatening condition and we have to operate now," or: "You have a life-threatening condition but we'll decide whether we need to operate next week." The Federal Reserve, with its 75 basis points interest rate cut, has sent a signal that it is truly concerned and will do what it takes to avert deflation. But no aggressive intervention is without risk. 

There has now been enough weak US economic data to justify much looser monetary policy. Surveys of consumer and business sentiment suggest a sharp slowdown; there is evidence that credit is now harder to obtain; and there are signs of incipient labour market weakness. All support lower rates, although most will have been anticipated when the Fed last met in December. 
   TDWhat is new, and what may have pushed the Fed to act, is the woes of so-called "monoline" insurers. Their failure, which is now a present danger, could produce more financial contagion and reduce credit to a swath of the real economy. There is an overall danger that successive blows to confidence could create a recession where none was in prospect, and the Fed's sharp rate cut is an important counteraction. 

It may not be decisive, however. Banks are running scared of credit losses and it remains to be seen whether they will expand their lending, or even pass all of the Fed's cut on to their customers. The effects of lower rates may be limited - but that is an argument for cutting more rather than less. 

That it is an "emergency" cut - made just one week before a scheduled meeting - risks creating the impression that the Fed has panicked. It will also add to perceptions that the Fed is susceptible to market pressure (equities fell heavily on Monday) and that the mode of its action is unpredictable. Both will make monetary policy harder to conduct in normal times. 

The risk to inflation is clear but, at this point, worth taking. The core consumer price index rose by 2.4 per cent in the year to December and the headline index, including food and energy, rose much more. In normal circumstances this would be worrying, but while the headlines are about collapsing house prices and the risk of recession, there is little chance of igniting a spiral of higher wages and prices. 

The Fed needs to explain why it could not wait a week but, on balance, aggressive rate cuts are a risk worth running. It must also accept the inverse of today's policy: that when the economy stabilises, rates will have to rise as fast as they fell. The Fed's skill as an economic doctor is about to be tested.
