 HDUPDATE 2-Thailand says U.S. cut a factor for rate policy      BYBy Boontiwa Wichakul      WC472 words   PD23 January 2008   ET07:45   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds analyst comments, market reaction)   

BANGKOK, Jan 23 (Reuters) - The surprise rate cut by the U.S. Federal Reserve will be a factor in setting Thai interest rates, a senior central banker said on Wednesday, stirring expectations Thailand may trim its borrowing costs again.   
   TD"Our monetary policy is independent from the Fed. However, we will take into account the Fed fund rate too," Deputy Bank of Thailand Governor Atchana Waiquamdee told reporters a day after the Fed slashed its benchmark rate by 75 basis points.   

"Nobody knows for sure about the direction of capital movements," Atchana said.   

"If people think the U.S. economy will be bad after the rate cut, money may flow out of the United States to other countries. It depends on how much each country is linked to the U.S. economy."   

Bond yields <0#THTSY=> <0#THBMK=> fell by 20-25 basis points across the curve in the secondary market on Wednesday as investors saw Atchana's comments as suggesting interest rate cuts, dealers said.   

The Bank of Thailand has kept rates steady at 3.25 percent since July 2007 following a series of cuts, and in the past few weeks expectations grew rates would stay on hold for months to come and the next move was likely to be up.   

But the Fed's emergency move just a week before its regular meeting and the Thai central banker's comments made analysts start factoring in another rate cut.   

"In our view, there is already a clear sign that the BoT's monetary policy has now shifted to an easing bias," Standard Chartered Bank economist Usara Wilaipich said.   

"The hasty rate cut by the Fed should provide room and flexibility for the BoT to consider cutting rates sooner than expected in order to boost growth and reduce the baht appreciation," she said.   

Economist Vishnu Varathan of Forecast in Singapore agreed a rate easing was a possibility though Thailand's policymakers should not act in a knee-jerk fashion.   

"There seems to be room for a 25-50 bps cut if push comes to shove. But certainly, Thailand cannot look to move in lock-step with the Fed," he said.   

The Bank of Thailand will meet on Feb. 27 to review its policy.   

At 0900 GMT, the baht was little changed at 33.12/15 to the dollar onshore <THB=TH> and at 30.75/95 offshore <THB=W>.   

The Thai stock market, which was down 0.12 percent at midday before the central banker's remarks, was up 0.8 percent at 0900 GMT. (Additional reporting and writing by Orathai Sriring; Editing by Tomasz Janowski)

 HDUPDATE 1-INTERVIEW-Saudi Fransi says rate cuts hurt bank profits      BYBy Souhail Karam      WC619 words   PD23 January 2008   ET11:19   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Updates with quotes, background, details)   

RIYADH, Jan 23 (Reuters) -  Banque Saudi Fransi said interest rate cuts and lower brokerage income had hurt earnings in the fourth quarter after missing analysts' forecasts with its smallest profit since a 2006 stock market crash.   
   TDBanque Saudi Fransi was among four of the five largest Saudi banks to miss profit forecasts in the fourth quarter when the Saudi central bank slashed its deposit rate by 100 basis points to deter bets on the appreciation of its dollar-pegged riyal.   

The central bank also raised its reserve requirement for the first time in 27 years on Nov. 1, forcing banks to keep more money in their vaults to prevent lower interest rates from stoking inflation.   

The lower interest rates hit bank earnings by slashing the return lenders in world's largest oil exporter get on their funds as record crude prices bought cash pouring into the country, Saudi Fransi's Chief Financial Officer said on Tuesday.   

"We are awash with liquidity, so we put it in the money market," Philippe Touchard told Reuters in an interview on Tuesday.   

"But cuts in interest rates slashed returns. The more we are liquid, the less money we make when the rates are declining," he said.   

The Saudi central bank cut its deposit rate, the reverse repurchase rate, by a further 50 basis points on Wednesday after the U.S. Federal Reserve slashed borrowing costs by three quarters of a percentage point on Tuesday. The central bank also raised the reserve requirement to 10 percent of deposits from 9 percent.   

SMALLEST QUARTERLY PROFIT   

Saudi Fransi, 31 percent owned by France's Credit Agricole , made a net profit of 640.5 million riyals ($170.8 million) in the three months to Dec. 31, missing even the most pessimistic forecast in a Reuters survey last month.   

It was the bank's smallest quarterly profit since 2006 when the Saudi stock market lost half its value between February and July, slashing brokerage revenue and asset management revenue.   

Fransi's brokerage business, the kingdom's third largest, managed to retain market share of around 12 percent, but stock market related income fell 61 percent to 589 million riyals in 2007, Touchard said.   

That was because of decreasing trading volumes, he said, declining to give figures for the fourth quarter. Saudi companies publish detailed quarterly data about a week after releasing a headline figure.   

The Saudi benchmark, the worst performer among Arab markets in 2006, rallied almost 43 percent in the fourth quarter, better than any other in the region. Touchard said income from stock-market related business was hurt by around two weeks of holidays to mark the end of Islam's month-long Ramadan fast and the annual haj pilgrimage season.   

"In addition to this, volumes of transactions in the stock market were much higher when it was going down than they were when the bourse was recovering," he said. Trading volumes have fallen about 60 percent between 2006 and 2007, he said.   

Saudi Fransi, the kingdom's fourth-largest lender by market value, increased income from its core banking business by 28 percent to 3.1 billion riyals in 2007, Touchard said.   

"This shows that the kingdom has not been affected by the repercussions of the subprime crisis," he said of the global banking losses linked to defaults on U.S. home loans to the less creditworthy.   

"We are totally immune to the subprime crisis. We have not invested anything at all in these products," Touchard said. (Editing by Will Waterman)

 HDIndian rate swaps at 2-yr lows after U.S. rate cuts      WC198 words   PD23 January 2008   ET11:27   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPMUMBAI, Jan 23 (Reuters) - Indian overnight indexed swaps eased to fresh two-year lows on Wednesday, after the U.S. Federal Reserve cut rates, raising expectations the Reserve Bank of India could follow suit to lower the interest rate differential.   

At 3:50 p.m., the five-year swap rate <INROIS> was at 6.44/6.46 percent, which was its lowest since Jan. 18, 2006.   
   TD"Sentiment has turned very bullish after the Fed rate cuts," a trader with a foreign bank, said.   

The Fed cut interest rates by 75 basis points to 3.5 percent on Tuesday in a surprise move and dealers said the widening rate differential may create arbitrage opportunities and increase capital inflows, pressuring the rupee higher.   

To cap its gains, India's central bank has been buying dollars flooding the system with inflation-fuelling excess cash.   

RBI Governor Yaga Venugopal Reddy said earlier this week the risk of a U.S. recession would be an important factor at the policy review on Jan. 29. (Reporting by Anurag Joshi; Editing by Prem Udayabhanu)

 HDDAVOS-US Treasury official says extensive intl cooperation      BYBy Mike Dolan      WC599 words   PD23 January 2008   ET21:52   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPDAVOS, Switzerland, Jan 23 (Reuters) - The U.S. Treasury and Federal Reserve have had an enormous amount of cooperation with overseas counterparts on recent economy-boosting measures, the Treasury's top international diplomat said on Wednesday.   

In an interview with Reuters on the sidelines of the annual World Economic Forum in Davos, Treasury Under Secretary for International Affairs David McCormick said he was puzzled by concern at the Davos meeting about a lack of coordination on the recent financial and economic turmoil.   
   TD"There has been an enormous amount of cooperation that has taken place within the context of near-term policy decisions," McCormick told Reuters in the Swiss ski resort where some 2,500 business and government leaders are meeting this week.   

Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson and the Fed have extensive contact with their counterparts among the Group of Seven economic powers and elsewhere on short-term policies, he said, referring to the Fed's dramatic interest rate cut this week and last week's plans for a U.S. fiscal stimulus.   

"At the G7, these will not be people who have not talked about these problems since they last met," he said, referring to the G7 finance chiefs' meeting in Tokyo next month.   

"I don't want to prejudge what would be in any (G7) communique but this (the current financial and economic concerns) will be the centrepoint of any discussions."   

McCormick said the G7's Financial Stability Forum, in conjunction with the International Monetary Fund, was also involved in a key international effort to address the root causes and appropriate responses to the credit crunch that is sapping economic growth.   

The FSF, under Bank of Italy chief Mario Draghi, is expected to make an "interim report" to the February G7 meeting, he said, and that will focus on greater credit market transparency, market liquidity, the role of credit ratings agencies, and financial supervision.   

FASTER YUAN RISE   

McCormick said the role of China and other leading emerging economies was also just as important, given the growth of their share of the global economy.   

As part of helping a better balance in the world economy, he urged China to sustain the recent acceleration of the yuan's rise against the dollar and other currencies and its moves to make the yuan exchange rate more market-based.   

Referring to a more flexible yuan as still a top priority, McCormick added: "We have seen movement there."   

"The (Chinese) currency has appreciated nominally about 14.5 percent since July 2005 and on trade-weighted terms about six percent," he said. "And in the last 90 days we've seen even greater appreciation ."   

"We would just encourage the Chinese to continue to maintain that accelerated pace."   

Elsewhere, McCormick said the United States welcomed investment from huge sovereign wealth funds set up mostly by developing countries to recycle windfalls from export- or commodity-led economic booms.   

As long as the investments remained long-term and commercially, not politically, focused, there was nothing to worry about the recent stakes taken by Middle Eastern and Asian funds in big U.S. banks, he said.   

The IMF should merely develop a list of guidelines and best practices for these funds and the supervisory bodies of the receiving countries, McCormick added.   

For full coverage, blogs and TV from Davos see:   http://uk.reuters.com/news/globalcoverage/worldeconomicforum2008   (Reporting by Mike Dolan, Editing by Chizu Nomiyama)

 HDTREASURIES-Bonds rally on global equity sell-off      BYBy Richard Leong      WC423 words   PD22 January 2008   ET13:41   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPNEW YORK, Jan 22 (Reuters) - U.S. Treasuries rallied on Tuesday, as benchmark yields fell to 4-1/2 year lows on a stampede into safe-haven assets and speculations that the Federal Reserve may cut interest rates prior to next week's policy meeting.   

Growing fears of a worldwide slowdown led by the possibility of a U.S. recession have rattled investor confidence, driven money out of stocks and riskier assets into the relative safety and liquidity of U.S. government debt.   
   TD"People are looking at extreme safety, and that's U.S. Treasuries. It's really the last man standing," said Doug Roberts, chief investment strategist at Channel Capital Research in Shrewsbury, New Jersey.   

U.S. stock futures are expected to open sharply lower following heavy losses in Japan and a roller-coaster session so far in Europe.   

Dow Jones Industrial average futures shed more than 500 points after the U.S. financial markets were shut on Monday for the Martin Luther King holiday. For more details see [ID:nN22468100].   

The sharp downturn in global equities, together with protracted problems plaguing the U.S. housing and mortgage markets, have spurred bets that the Fed would be forced to pare the key federal funds rate prior to its Jan. 29-30 meeting.   

U.S. interest rate futures suggested that traders were pricing in the likelihood that Fed would lower the borrowing cost of surplus reserves between banks at least three-quarter of a percentage point by next week.   

Given the lack of major economic data and Fed official speeches this week, Treasuries will be tied closely to the stock market, analysts said.   

The flight into ultra-safe investment pushed the two-year Treasuries yield <US2YT=RR> briefly below 2 percent for the first time in nearly four years in overseas trading.   

The two-year Treasury yield is now more than two percentage points below overnight rates, underscoring the expectations for an aggressive Fed response to help the economy.   

Benchmark 10-year note's price <US10YT=RR> was up 1-5/32, coming off from a 1-24/32 gain in overseas trading. Its yield which moves inversely with its price was 3.492 percent, down 15 basis points from Friday, after sagging to 3.473 percent, its lowest since mid-2003. (Reporting by Richard Leong; Editing by Theodore d'Afflisio)

 HDPaulson calls Fed emergency rate cut "constructive"      WC127 words   PD22 January 2008   ET14:44   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPWASHINGTON, Jan 22 (Reuters) - U.S. Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson said on Tuesday that the Federal Reserve's emergency interest rate cut was constructive and should help boost confidence amid volatile world financial markets.   

"This is very constructive and I think it shows this country and the rest of the world that our central bank is nimble and can move quickly in response to market conditions," Paulson said.   
   TDThe Fed slashed benchmark U.S. interest rates by a hefty three-quarters of a percentage point on Tuesday in an effort to support a weak U.S. economy. (Editing by Tom Hals)

 HDINSTANT VIEW 3-Fed cuts target rate in surprise move by 75 bps      WC765 words   PD22 January 2008   ET14:47   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP NEW YORK, Jan 22 (Reuters) - The U.S. Federal Reserve on Tuesday slashed the benchmark federal funds target rate by 75 basis points to 3.5 percent in a surprise intermeeting decision.   

 Story: [ID:nWAT008737] Text: [ID:nN22475900]   
   TD COMMENTS:   

 MATTHEW SHARRATT, ECONOMIST, BANK OF AMERICA, LONDON:   

 "It's certainly a surprise move. The Fed has clearly emphasised the downside risks to growth and felt it needed to counteract this with an immediate 75 basis point move.   

 "We cannot discount the Fed will have to move further. Whether this will be enough to fend off the headwinds the U.S. we will have to see."   

 MARK MEADOWS, CURRENCY STRATEGIST, TEMPUS CONSULTING, WASHINGTON:   

 "Under any other Fed this would not be a surprise, but this Fed has been reluctant to cater to market expectations. This should support the euro in the short term, however our longstanding view is still that the U.S. economy will rebound and help the dollar gain into the middle of this year."   

 PETER BOOCKVAR, EQUITY STRATEGIST, MILLER TABAK & CO., NEW YORK:   

 "The inter-meeting was unexpected, but Fed funds futures were saying they were going to cut 75 basis points next week. So we got that today instead of next Wednesday. This was obviously a panic relief type of move."   

 RON SIMPSON, DIRECTOR OF CURRENCY RESEARCH, ACTION ECONOMCIS, TAMPA, FLORIDA:   

 "This is a gutless move by the Fed. If anything this move just fuels panic trades further. And the markets are pricing in more rate cuts next week. The dollar's reaction was mixed after the Fed decision -- it was lower vs the euro but higher against the yen because equity futures shot up. Ultimately, this is bad for the dollar as the markets try top price in a gutless U.S. central bank."   

 KEVIN FLANAGAN, FIXED INCOME STRATEGIST FOR GLOBAL WEALTH MANAGEMENT WITH MORGAN STANLEY IN PURCHASE, NEW YORK:   

 "You are seeing Treasuries sell off a bit."   

 "I am not really sure if the Fed moving a week ahead of time does anything from an economic standpoint, but what they are trying to do is address heightened levels of anxiety in the financial market."   

 BOB MACINTOSH, CHIEF ECONOMIST AT EATON VANCE:   

 "This is a surprise, but then again considering what has been happening in the last 48 hours, it is really not a surprise. (The Fed) realizes it is behind the curve. This is more of a psychological move than anything else with the Fed clearly trying to tell us that they are giving us a hug. It should help turn things around. Let's see."   

 JOHN TIERNEY, ANALYST, DEUTSCHE BANK, NEW YORK:   

 "The Fed is very, very, very worried. For now it's going to put a floor on the market. Markets may perk up a bit until it figures out what this means."   

 DUSTIN REID, CURRENCY STRATEGIST, ABN AMRO, CHICAGO:   

 "The Fed is going to be there for liquidity purposes no matter what. They weren't prepared to wait for next week. There's a Bank of Canada meeting this morning so maybe there is a bit of coordination of cutting. It's good news for local market currencies and good news for high-yielding currencies."   

 WILLIAM O'DONNELL, DIRECTOR OF INTEREST RATE STRATEGY, UBS SECURITIES LLC, NEW YORK:   

 "This should stem some of the bleeding in stocks. If stocks roar back, it will be negative for yields. The Fed seems committed to get the economy off the floor."   

 MICHAEL METZ, CHIEF INVESTMENT STRATEGIST AT OPPENHEIMER & CO IN NEW YORK:   

 "That should moderate the decline, but frankly I think we're in for a forced liquidiation phase which has further to run, maybe another day or two.   

 The Fed is obviously panicked, but unfortunately they have no power to reverse what in my opinion is the worst post-war recession.   

 "I hate to be cute, but I think we're in for wild swings. I'd look to buy on weakness late in afternoon or on early tomororrow on another wave of forced liquidation."   

 MARKET REACTION:   

 - BONDS: U.S. Treasuries pared gains. They had been sharply higher earlier in the session in a flight-to-safety bid amid a global equities sell off.   

 - CURRENCIES: The dollar fell sharply It was higher beforehand.   

 - US STOCKS: U.S. equity index futures moved off session lows. Futures were dramatically lower beforehand. They still point to a drop in U.S. stock indexes when the market opens at 9:30 ET (1430 GMT)

 HDChilean stocks bounce back after U.S. rate cut      WC98 words   PD22 January 2008   ET14:34   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited

 HDUS RATE FUTURES-See 50 bps cut likely at FOMC meet      WC282 words   PD22 January 2008   ET14:36   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds details)   

CHICAGO, Jan 22 (Reuters) - U.S. short-term interest rate futures rallied sharply on Tuesday in anticipation of more Federal Reserve rate cuts this month after the Fed stepped in to slash its benchmark lending rate by 75 basis points.   
   TDThe Fed's emergency rate cut, more than a week before its scheduled policy meeting, lowered the fed funds rate to 3.5 percent at a time many fear the U.S. economy is on the verge of, or might have already entered, a recession.   

Futures price another 25 basis point rate cut at the Federal Open Market Committee's Jan 29-30 meeting. They also show a 78 percent chance the Fed will cut by another 50 basis points, which would put fed funds at 3 percent.   

In a statement, the FOMC said it moved "in view of a weakening of the economic outlook and increasing downside risks to growth."   

The Chicago Fed's national activity index, also released on Tuesday, was weaker in December and very close to flashing a recession signal, reflecting broad-based weakness across a range of indicators.   

"The economic data will continue to worsen; this move is not an instant fix. The economy is still staring recession in the face, but at least the Fed now gets it," said Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics in Valhalla, New York.   

Futures suggest deeper rate cuts ahead over the next few months, taking fed funds by mid-year to about 2.25 percent.

 HDWRAPUP 2-ECB holds tough line on inflation, no comment on Fed      BYBy Henrique Almeida and Ingrid Melander      WC899 words   PD22 January 2008   ET16:58   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds remarks by ECB's Papademos, Stark; Eurogroup's Juncker)   

BRUSSELS/LISBON, Jan 22 (Reuters) - European Central Bank policymakers held their tough line on inflation risks on Tuesday despite fresh turmoil on financial markets and an emergency cut in U.S. interest rates.   
   TDThe ECB declined to comment on the U.S. Federal Reserve's 75-basis-point interest rate cut, which took U.S rates below the euro zone's for the first time since late 2004 and surprised some people at the ECB as well as market participants.   

But Luxembourg Prime Minister Jean-Claude Juncker, who sits in on ECB Governing Council meetings, said he did not expect the ECB to follow suit, and comments by ECB policymakers suggested there was no consensus for such a move.   

Executive Board members Juergen Stark and Jose Manuel Gonzalez-Paramo said the euro zone's economic fundamentals were sound, and pointed to continued upward pressure on inflation, a stance echoed by German Bundesbank President Axel Weber.   

Nonetheless, there was some division on the economic outlook. Portuguese central bank governor Vitor Constancio said European growth could be slower than earlier expected, comments which were in line with growing market expectations that the ECB may have to change tack before the end of the year.   

The ECB has kept interest rates at 4.0 percent since mid-2007 but economists believe the Governing Council is increasingly squeezed by the prospect of slower growth on the one hand and price risks on the other.   

"Our main concern is the high inflation rate of 3.1 percent" in the euro zone, Stark said, adding that the ECB was "very worried and alarmed" about inflation in an interview with German newspaper Die Zeit conducted on Tuesday after the Fed decision.   

Earlier, giving a speech in Brussels, Stark also passed up on the opportunity to comment on the rate cuts by the Fed and the  Bank of Canada, saying he had only just received the news and did not know all the details. "I have to talk to my colleagues," he told reporters.   

But Juncker told Luxembourg television later in the day that he did not think the ECB would follow the Fed's lead.   

"The central bank is right to advocate price stability. I don't expect the ECB to take a similar rate step as the American central bank, meaning to adjust rates downwards," he said.   

INFLATION VS GROWTH   

Gonzalez-Paramo said the ECB would, as always, do what was needed to protect price stability, but that it was not possible to say how market turmoil would influence inflation. For its part, growth was still holding up.   

"The main scenario is growth consistent with potential, which is around two percent," he told Portuguese broadcaster SIC before the Fed announcement. "The fundamentals of the European economy remain solid."   

Weber said the ECB would not allow current inflation at 6-1/2-year highs to spill over into wage demands and consumer prices. "We have zero tolerance for a wage-price spiral. If financial and wage developments threaten to leave the stability corridor, we will act decisively," he said in an interview with German magazine Focus Money.   

ECB Vice President Lucas Papademos, speaking in Luxembourg, also said it was "more important than ever" that central banks did not take their eye off inflation.   

But he was guarded about the ECB's outlook for growth of around 2 percent this year, which three policymakers have called into doubt in the past week.   

"There is a lot of uncertainty ... for a few months we have stressed the existence of heightened uncertainty and downside risk."   

"It is not unimaginable that economic activity will be sustained at a level close to potential growth.   

Constancio struck a more cautious note on inflation, noting that this was also influenced by real economic developments, and said Europe would not be immune to the rising risk of a U.S. recession.   

"Certainly the probability has increased and that's a consequence of a revision of expectations by investors worldwide," he told Reuters during a conference in Lisbon.   

Stark and Weber played down the prospect of a U.S. recession, saying growth should continue albeit at a weaker pace.   

They also shrugged off renewed turbulence on financial markets, after European shares sank 6 percent on Monday, their biggest one-day slide since the Sept. 11, 2001 attacks amid concern about the scale of the U.S. economic slowdown and fears of more financial sector losses.   

"Financial markets tend to overreact," Gonzalez-Paramo said. "There is overshooting and undershooting. I would not make a full story out of what happened yesterday."   

Stark repeated earlier calls for more transparency and said the process of market correction would probably continue for a while.   

"The markets are very nervous," he said. "But we are seeing an overreaction. Fear and panic are bad advisers. We should not forget the facts. Fundamental economic data are good." (Additional reporting by Sergio Goncalves in Lisbon, Philip Blenkinsop and Michele Sinner in Luxembourg and Klaus Lauer and Iain Rogers in Berlin; writing by David Milliken and Krista Hughes; editing by David Stamp)

 HDWRAPUP 1- Bank of Canada opts for mild rate cut despite Fed      BYBy Louise Egan      WC710 words   PD22 January 2008   ET17:10   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2008 Reuters Limited   
   LPOTTAWA, Jan 22 (Reuters) - The  Bank of Canada held back in the face of an aggressive interest rate cut by the U.S. Federal Reserve on Tuesday, shaving just a quarter-point off its own key rate, but it signaled more cuts to come as U.S. recession worries spiral.   

In a regularly scheduled announcement, the central bank lowered its overnight lending rate to 4 percent from 4.25 percent. It was the bank's second straight quarter-point cut as it seeks to protect the Canadian economy from the severe slowdown or even recession that faces Canada's top trading partner.   
   TDThe move came shortly after the Fed slashed its federal funds rate by 75 basis points to 3.5 percent. It disappointed a growing number of market players who are calling for a bolder rate cut by Canada to match the Fed.   

"The  Bank of Canada is clearly stubborn, not wanting to cut by more than 25 basis points and I think the market is quite disappointed," said Eric Lascelles, chief economics and rates strategist at TD Securities.   

Ted Carmichael, chief economist at  JP Morgan Canada, said the bank "did what was expected a week ago and hasn't reacted whatsoever to developments in financial markets in the past week."   

The  Bank of Canada, which prepared its rate announcement on Monday, opted not to alter its decision at the last minute in reaction to the Fed move.   

While warning that Canada's exports will be hit by the U.S. slowdown, the bank emphasized that high commodity prices will continue to help keep Canada's economy buoyant.   

"Despite tighter credit conditions, domestic demand is projected to remain strong," the bank said in its statement.   

Retail sales for November rose by a stronger-than-expected 0.7 percent, new data showed on Tuesday.   

However, the bank left the door wide open to future rate cuts. "Further monetary stimulus is likely to be required in the near term to keep aggregate supply and demand in balance and to return inflation to target over the medium term."   

Given the massive selloff in world stock markets and the quickly-worsening outlook for the United States, Canada's central bank might consider cutting rates in between scheduled announcement dates, some analysts said.   

The Toronto stock index plunged 4.75 percent on Monday in its biggest drop in more than seven years, but turned higher on Tuesday.   

The bank has only made a surprise rate move once since adopting a fixed schedule for rate announcements in November 2000 -- a 50 basis point cut six days after the Sept. 11, 2001 attacks in the United States.   

DODGE'S LAST HURRAH   

The rate decision was the last under Governor David Dodge, who hands the baton to Mark Carney on Feb. 1, and some analysts believe the symbolism weighed into the bank's decision.   

"This is Dodge's last hurrah and he may have wished to pass things along in a somewhat conservative fashion and not commit on Carney's behalf without Carney really getting to take the reins here," Lascelles said.   

Hinting at revised projections scheduled for release on Thursday, the central bank said its 2008 outlook for the U.S. economy is now "significantly weaker" than the 2.1 percent it forecast in October. The Canadian economy will move into a situation of excess supply in the second quarter of this year, it said.   

However, it said the economy is still running at above capacity despite slowing in the fourth quarter.   

The bank's quarterly business survey showed the portion of firms with some degree of difficulty meeting increased demand shot up to a record high of 60 percent in the fourth quarter, a sign of inflationary pressure.   

Inflation projections are now being revised downward, with both total and core consumer inflation now expected to dip below 1.5 percent by mid-2008 before returning to the bank's 2 percent target by the end of 2009. (Reporting by Louise Egan; Editing by Peter Galloway)
